    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0dfe4b4bef9a4b4d83b23783ee602fed">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '669c1c20-6a1a-40ba-ba1b-36b5b66b3c08'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d127202414" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The volume collapse and slow kinetics reaction of anode materials are two key issues for sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, an “embryo” strategy is proposed for synthesis of nanorod–embedded MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C network nanoarchitecture as anode for SIBs with high-rate performance. Interestingly, L-cysteine which plays triple roles including sulfur source, reductant, and carbon source can be utilized to produce the sulfur vacancy–enriched heterostructure. Specifically, L-cysteine can combine with metastable monoclinic MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods at room temperature to encapsulate the “nutrient” of MoO<sub>x</sub> analogues (MoO<sub>2.5</sub>(OH)<sub>0.5</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>·0.5H<sub>2</sub>O) and hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine in the “embryo” precursor affording for subsequent in situ multistep heating treatment. The resultant MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C presents a high-rate capability of 875 and 420&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are much better than the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials reported by far. Finite element simulation and analysis results verify that the volume expansion can be reduced to 42.8% from 88.8% when building nanorod–embedded porous network structure. Theoretical calculations reveal that the sulfur vacancies and heterointerface engineering can promote the adsorption and migration of Na<sup>+</sup> leading to highly enhanced thermodynamic and kinetic reaction. The work provides an efficient approach to develop advanced electrode materials for energy storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307986-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307986-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been well developed and widely used, nevertheless, the growing scarcity of lithium reserves is hindering their further large-scale application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0001_R_d127202406e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are promising candidates due to their similar reaction mechanism to LIBs, abundant sodium reserves, and low costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0002_R_d127202406e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Rev. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.06&nbsp;Å) is much larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å), triggering slow Na<sup>+</sup> migration and large volume expansion during charge and discharge process, which is the major stumbling block for the development of high–efficiency anode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0003_R_d127202406e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Though carbon materials have exhibited considerable reversible capacities (200–300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and durability, they can hardly satisfy with the requirement of high energy densities and high capacities for advanced electronic devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0004_R_d127202406e554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Up to now, it is still of great challenge to develop anodes with high-rate capacity and long lifespan for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0005_R_d127202406e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Nano">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the anode candidates, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) is considered as a potential material for SIBs due to its low cost, wide layer spacing (0.62&nbsp;nm), and high theoretical capacity (670&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0006_R_d127202406e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy J. Energy Chem. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the common issues such as low reaction kinetics, large volume changes, and the dissolution of polysulphides during the cycling process usually cause electrochemical polarization and electrode crushing, thereby leading to poor rate performance and rapid capacity decay for the transition metal sulfide anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0007_R_d127202406e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater. Nano Energy Adv. Sci. eScience">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Introducing conductive carbon materials have been verified an efficient method to improve the above problems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0008_R_d127202406e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Small Appl. Surf. Sci. J. Colloid Interface Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater. Rare Met. Rare Met.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the issue of low intrinsic conductivity of MoS<sub>2</sub> active materials is still unsolved fundamentally, resulting in an imperfect sodium storage performance.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, vacancy engineering has been demonstrated an efficient approach to modulate the electronic structures for achieving increased conductivity and intrinsic active sites of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0009_R_d127202406e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Eng. J. Electrochim. Acta J. Alloys Compd. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Coord. Chem. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, it is found that vacancies in the transition metal chalcogenides can act as active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> embedding, while weakening the repulsion between adjacent layers and enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, thus promoting the electrochemical reaction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0010_R_d127202406e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano Coord. Chem. Rev. Carbon Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, interface engineering strategies, such as the construction of heterostructures, can not only generate stable nanostructures by coupling electrode materials with different bandgaps but also facilitate redox reactions by creating built-in electric fields at the heterogeneous interface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0006_R_d127202406e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy J. Energy Chem. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">6</a>, <a href="#smll202307986-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0011_R_d127202406e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, the non-homogeneous heterointerface formed by the oxides and sulphides can effectively reduce the irreversibility and ion transport potential of the electrode materials, so that accelerating the ion/electron transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0011_R_d127202406e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Chem. Eng. J.">11</a>, <a href="#smll202307986-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0012_R_d127202406e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, a dual heterostructure strategy was proposed to create strong interfacial interaction in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> material for accelerating the electrochemical reaction process resulting in highly enhanced capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0013_R_d127202406e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The rate performance was highly improved through the incorporation of Se vacancy and interface engineering in the Co<sub>0.85</sub>Se/WSe<sub>2</sub> heterostructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0010_R_d127202406e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano Coord. Chem. Rev. Carbon Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, rare research reports the synthesis of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for high-rate capacity and durability at 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, because of the inevitable volume change and slow mesoscale electrolyte diffusion in the process of taking off or embedding Na<sup>+</sup> at high current density. Interestingly, the conductive carbon mesh with its porous structure can both facilitate the penetration and diffusion of electrolyte and alleviate the problem of volume expansion thus ensuring mass transfer and structure stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0014_R_d127202406e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Nano Res.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Taking into account the above-mentioned advantages, the composites those possess vacancies, heterointerfaces, and porous structures could deliver high intrinsic conductivity, rapid ion/electron migration, and quick diffusion, which will most likely endow the anodes with high-rate capacity and long-term stability. So far, however, it still remains a challenge for the construction of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode heterostructures that couples with sulfur vacancies and porous carbon network due to the lack of efficient synthesis approach.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, an “embryo” strategy is proposed to produce MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C heterostructure for robust SIBs with the assistance of trifunctional L-cysteine. The “embryo” precursor is first prepared via incorporating L-cysteine with metastable MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods at room temperature (RT), and then is performed at high temperature via a multistep heating process to in situ generate the nanorod–embedded and sulfur vacancy–enriched network nanoarchitecture. According to the Finite element simulation and analysis (FEA) and density functional theory (DFT) results, the volume expansion, the thermodynamic adsorption, and kinetic migration of Na<sup>+</sup> are highly improved, which can be attributed to the particular configuration and composition. As a result, the rate capacity and cycling stability of sodium storage are significantly boosted in the optimized MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C network nanoarchitecture.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307986-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307986-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Based on the characterization results of field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images, and X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), the metastable MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods with monoclinic phase and monocrystalline structure were successfully prepared. The schematic diagram for the preparation of the “embryo” precursors was displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1a</a>. Specifically, the monoclinic MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods and L-cysteine were dispersed in the deionized water and stirred at RT to produce a group of precursors via changing the stirring time to 2 (Precursor-2&nbsp;h), 4 (Precursor-4&nbsp;h), and 8&nbsp;h (Precursor-8&nbsp;h), respectively. Seen from the FESEM images shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,d</a> (Supporting Information), the Precursor-2&nbsp;h maintained rod-like morphology except for the formation of sharp points at the both ends. When prolonging the stirring time to 4&nbsp;h, a thin film adhered onto the surface of sharp nanorods (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1c,f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b,e</a>, Supporting Information), and the nanorods were totally wrapped in the thick film after increasing the stirring time to 8&nbsp;h (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c,f</a>, Supporting Information). The change of the morphology signified that the nanorods could be corroded and coated by L-cysteine in different degrees forming the discrepant “embryo” precursors via regulating the stirring time. Furthermore, as seen from the comparison of XRD patterns, peaks at 12.9°, 25.8°, and 39.0° belonging to the metastable MoO<sub>3</sub> disappeared, while new peaks corresponding to the stable orthorhombic MoO<sub>2.5</sub>(OH)<sub>0.5</sub> (JCPDS#14-0041) and MoO<sub>3</sub>·0.5H<sub>2</sub>O (JCPDS#37-0519) could be identified in the as-prepared precursors (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1e</a>). The XRD results revealed that the H from the L-cysteine was doped in the MoO<sub>3</sub> at RT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0015_R_d127202406e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. RSC Adv.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, three peaks at 18.2°, 24.5°, and 36.9° which showed slight shift compared with those of the standard position of L-cysteine emerged in the Precursor-4&nbsp;h and grew stronger in the Precursor-8&nbsp;h, verifying the hydrogen-deficiency of L-cysteine in the precursors. According to the thermal analysis and thermal gravimetric analysis (TG-DTA) of Precursor-4&nbsp;h in N<sub>2</sub>, three apparent exothermic peaks were identified (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0001">1f</a>). Specifically, the first exothermic maximum was located at 183°C, which could be attributed to the removal of adsorbed and crystal water (MoO<sub>3</sub>·0.5H<sub>2</sub>O); the sharp exothermic peak with major weight loss at 230°C could be described as the decomposition of MoO<sub>2.5</sub>(OH)<sub>0.5</sub> and hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine; the third weight loss at 460°C with a broad temperature region (300–600°C) could be due to the combustion or carbonization of the decomposed materials. The Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) indicated that the typical S─H vibration at 2550&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> was gradually weak and disappeared in the precursors accompanied by the increase of S─S vibration at 605&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, when prolonging the stirring time from 2 to 8&nbsp;h. The FT-IR results revealed that the H from the S─H group of L-cysteine could break resulting in the formation of hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine (HOOCCH(NH<sub>2</sub>)CH<sub>2</sub>-S─S-CH<sub>2</sub>CH(NH<sub>2</sub>)COOH), and the quantities of the hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine in the precursors can be modulated by changing stirring time. The above FESEM images, XRD patterns, TG-DTA results, and FT-IR analysis together indicated L-cysteine could combine with metastable MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods to encapsulate the resultant “nutrient” of MoO<sub>x</sub> analogues (MoO<sub>2.5</sub>(OH)<sub>0.5</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>·0.5H<sub>2</sub>O) and hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine in the “embryo” precursors at RT, which were served for the subsequent in situ multistep heating treatment.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/da6d1cbf-a15f-420a-b957-8e052a9f3296/smll202307986-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/da6d1cbf-a15f-420a-b957-8e052a9f3296/smll202307986-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/28a44c7c-7feb-424d-aa0b-466e7ab28344/smll202307986-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/da6d1cbf-a15f-420a-b957-8e052a9f3296/smll202307986-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram for the preparation of “embryo” precursors. FESEM images of the b) Precursors-2&nbsp;h, c) Precursors-4&nbsp;h, and d) Precursors-8&nbsp;h, respectively. e) The comparison of XRD patterns for the precursors, MoO3, and L-cysteine raw. f) TG-DTA curves of the Precursor-4&nbsp;h.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The preparation diagrams of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information). The synthesis process of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanoarchitecture involves a multistep annealing treatment of the Precursor-4&nbsp;h in argon atmosphere. By contrast, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C can be produced by changing the “embryo” precursors and annealing technique, respectively. The details can be found in the supporting information. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2b</a>, the diffraction peaks at 26.0°, 37.0°, and 53.1° belonging to the (−111), (111), and (220) facets of MoO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS#73-1807) can be found in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C presented typical peaks at 14.3°, 32.8°, and 58.3° of MoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS #37-1492). Compared to that of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, the slight deviation of (002) facet could be attributed to the bending of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. In addition, the as-prepared materials showed bread peaks at around 21°, which can be attributed to the amorphous carbon. The XRD results verified that the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub> /C were successfully prepared. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b–e</a> (Supporting Information), the as-prepared MoO<sub>2</sub>/C showed rod-like morphology with smooth surface, while no network structure was found because of the lack of “nutrient” in the precursor. Benefiting from the abundant “nutrient” pre-encapsulated in the “embryo”, the L-cysteine could be adequately carbonized onto the nanorods, and at the same time in situ reduce and sulfur the MoO<sub>x</sub> analogues, thereby generating the nanorod–embedded MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C porous network (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). By contrast, the nanorods disappeared and the carbon with thick layer was stacked when there was excess “nutrient” in the precursor (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b–e</a>, Supporting Information). Seen from the TEM images of MoO<sub>2</sub>/C shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the nanorods presented smooth surface and typical (−111) facet of MoO<sub>2</sub> with lattice spacing of 0.34&nbsp;nm, and thin layered amorphous carbon was found outside the nanorods (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c,d</a>, Supporting Information). TEM images of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C showed that nanorods of ca. 120&nbsp;nm in diameter were embedded in the carbon nets forming the porous network (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2e</a>, Supporting Information). Two different lattice distances of 0.62 and 0.34&nbsp;nm which were corresponding to the (002) and (−111) facets of MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2f</a>) were identified, respectively. In addition, it could be found that the dark-contrast MoO<sub>2</sub> with regular lattices were coated by layered MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets generating an apparent heterogeneous interface (yellow dashed line) between MoO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>, according to the TEM and HRTEM images shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2f,g</a> (Supporting Information). Seen from the high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) and corresponding Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopic (EDS) mapping images of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0002">2h</a>, the Mo and S elements with wide color mapping were uniformly distributed in the nanorod and O element showed narrow color distribution, signifying that the MoS<sub>2</sub> could cover outside the MoO<sub>2</sub>. By contrast, thin MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with 2–3 layers and randomly distributed lattices were free-standing on the porous and amorphous carbon (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) forming the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C network. The MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-3&nbsp;h and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-6&nbsp;h composites were also produced via annealing the Precursor-3&nbsp;h and Precursor-6&nbsp;h, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). According to the carbon sulfur analyzer and TG-DTA results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), the weight percentages of carbon, MoO<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub> in the composites can be efficiently modulated via annealing the different precursors, and the quantities are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e2937a4c-6379-4654-8632-8ae5153c78c0/smll202307986-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e2937a4c-6379-4654-8632-8ae5153c78c0/smll202307986-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1c0599db-9979-436c-9313-41d768014553/smll202307986-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e2937a4c-6379-4654-8632-8ae5153c78c0/smll202307986-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram for the preparation of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C network nanoarchitecture. b) The comparison of XRD patterns for as-prepared MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. c,d) FESEM, e,f) TEM, g) HRTEM images, h) HAADF image and corresponding EDS mapping images of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, respectively. The scale bar in h is 100&nbsp;nm.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The isotherms of the as-prepared samples showed type IV adsorption isotherms indicating their porous feature (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0003">3a</a>). The specific surface area values were calculated to be 49, 93, and 13&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, respectively. From insert of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0003">3a</a>, the pores were concentrated at the mesoporous range in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, while the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/C exhibited macropore feature. The average pore sizes of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoO<sub>2</sub>/C were calculated to be ca. 18, 17, and 14&nbsp;nm, respectively. Seen from the survey X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a</a>, Supporting Information), the C, Mo, S, and O elements were identified in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. According to the comparison of high-resolution Mo 3d spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0003">3b</a>), the peaks belonging to the Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of MoS<sub>2</sub> at 229.4 and 232.51&nbsp;eV showed a positive shift in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C in comparison with those in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and a similar deviation could be seen in the S 2p spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b</a>, Supporting Information). Meanwhile, a blue shift for the high-resolution Mo 3d and O 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c</a>, Supporting Information) was concluded in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C when comparing with the peaks at 230.32 and 233.5&nbsp;eV in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C. From the high-resolution XPS spectra of C 1s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>, Supporting Information), the peak belonging to the C─N bond could be derived from L-cysteine. The XPS results verified that there could be the existence of electrons transfer from MoS<sub>2</sub> to MoO<sub>2</sub> endowing the heterostructure with strong interface interaction. The UV–vis absorption spectra of the as-prepared materials were collected to further verify the formation of heterostructure in the composite (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information). From Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a> (Supporting Information), the bandgap of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C was calculated to be 1.46&nbsp;eV, which was between those of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C (1.88&nbsp;eV) and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (1.27&nbsp;eV), signifying that there could be a strong electronic coupling between MoO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>. The electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) data delivered an apparent signal with approximate intensity at g = 2.003, meaning that abundant sulfur vacancies generated in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0003">3c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0016_R_d127202406e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectra of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0003">3d</a>) showed two peaks at 377 and 403&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> which were ascribed to the typical E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> and A<sub>1g</sub> vibration modes of MoS<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0017_R_d127202406e1150" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Small">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> meanwhile, two characteristic peaks at 206 and 237&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> assigning to the stretching modes of the Mo─O─Mo in the MoO<sub>2</sub> were observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0018_R_d127202406e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nanotechnology">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, peaks at 1360 and 1589&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to the D and G bands of carbon could be identified in all as-prepared materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/449b1e33-eec0-44c0-8df8-17b9962f924c/smll202307986-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/449b1e33-eec0-44c0-8df8-17b9962f924c/smll202307986-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6962c26d-e71d-4af8-af68-07ee4153afe5/smll202307986-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/449b1e33-eec0-44c0-8df8-17b9962f924c/smll202307986-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm and pore size distribution, b) high-resolution XPS spectra of Mo 3d, c) EPR data, and d) Raman spectra of MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium storage performances of as-prepared MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anodes were studied using coin-type cells. According to the cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4a</a>), the reduction peaks at 1.2 and 0.75&nbsp;V which can be ascribed to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the MoO<sub>2</sub> and the conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> into Mo + Na<sub>2</sub>S at the initial reduction reaction, respectively. The chemical reaction related to these peaks can be represented by the following chemical equations:

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ebf06170-bbe7-4753-9db1-0ca5f12ce35e/smll202307986-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ebf06170-bbe7-4753-9db1-0ca5f12ce35e/smll202307986-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05fa33b2-4992-45a9-954c-55de8468e56a/smll202307986-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ebf06170-bbe7-4753-9db1-0ca5f12ce35e/smll202307986-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves and b) charge and discharge plots of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C at the first three cycles. c) Rate-performances of MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. d) The comparison of rate capacities for the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes in the literature. The comparison of the cycling stabilities and corresponding Coulombic efficiency for the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C at e) 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and f) 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The peak at 0.87&nbsp;V could be attributed to the formation of solid electrolyte intermediate (SEI) film. During the initial oxidation, the peaks at 1.32 and 1.82&nbsp;V were related to the oxidation of Na<sub>x</sub>MoO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>x</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to MoO<sub>2</sub> and MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0019_R_d127202406e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interface Mater. Today Sustain.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The highly overlapped CV curves in the second and third circles signified the good reversibility and stability of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, as well as the good reversibility of the Na<sup>+</sup> storage reaction. Similar reaction processes were found in the CV tests of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), except for the poor overlap curves in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C. Specifically, from the CV tests of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information), the initial reduction peak at 0.99&nbsp;V is strong, which could be assigned to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the MoS<sub>2</sub>. The corresponding reaction process is as follows:

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202307986-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ff5468c1-c49c-4169-b429-0480a04d96d4/smll202307986-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202307986:smll202307986-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202307986-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>MoS</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>

                              <mi>Na</mi>

                           </msub>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{MoS}}_{\mathrm{2}}\,{\mathrm{ + }}\,{\mathrm{x}}\,{\mathrm{Na}}^{\mathrm{ + }}{\mathrm{ + }}\,{\mathrm{x}}\,{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - \to {\mathrm{Na}}_{\mathrm{x}}{\mathrm{MoS}}_{\mathrm{2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The peak at 0.83&nbsp;V relating to the formation of SEI film is less pronounced compared with the peak at 0.99&nbsp;V, which is consistent with the result reported in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0020_R_d127202406e1452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4b</a> showed the charge and discharge curves of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C for the first three cycles at 0.5 A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. It could be seen that the capacity reached to 1820&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the initial discharge, and there was an irreversible loss of capacity in the subsequent cycles because of the formation of SEI film. The charge and discharge curves for the second and third cycles were highly overlapped, indicating that cycling stability of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode was excellent, which was consistent with the CV results.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4c</a> displayed the rate performance of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anodes at different current densities. The average specific discharge capacities of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode were calculated to be 1027, 948, 875, 825, 742, 620, and 420&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which were much higher than those of MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/C at the same current densities. After reducing the current density from 10 to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the average specific discharge capacities of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C were highly reversible and maintained a stable capacity of 951&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Compared with the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C presented much higher rate capacity, especially at 5 and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4d</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The cycling stabilities of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anodes were first investigated at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4e</a>, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C maintained a discharge capacity of 801&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 4000 cycles with an average Coulombic efficiency of near 100%, showing a capacity retention rate of 82.1% (vs 2nd cycle). By contrast, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C delivered average discharge capacities of 246 and 486&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 4000 cycles, respectively, corresponding to capacity retention rates of 24.5% (vs 2nd cycle) and 53.5% (vs 2nd cycle). The MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C also retained a discharge capacity of 389&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles even at a high current condition of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, delivering a capacity retention rate of 86.4% in comparison with the stable capacity at the 10th cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0004">4f</a>). Compared with the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-3&nbsp;h and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C-6&nbsp;h electrodes with different active components, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C exhibited much better sodium storage performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The superior rate capacity and cycling stability of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode could be attributed to the synergy of nanorod–embedded porous network structure, heterogeneous interface, and rich sulfur vacancies which could avoid the stacking of electrode materials, accelerate ion/electron transport, and promote the kinetics reaction. The sodium-ion full battery which was consist of the anode of as-prepared MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and the cathode of commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> was further fabricated to investigate the practical applicability of the as-prepared anode material. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the full battery presented good cycling stability during 300 cycles with a discharge capacity of 131.7 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;at 0.5  A g<sup>−1</sup>, delivering a capacity retention rate of 91.4% (vs 20th cycle).</p>
         
         <p>To understand the reaction kinetic of the optimized MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, the CV measurements were conducted at 0.1–0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5a</a>, the curves kept their original shape with the increase of scanning rate, signifying the small polarization and excellent rate performance. According to the equation i = av<sup>b</sup>, the b values of the marked peaks were calculated to be 0.98, 0.85, 0.95, and 0.81, respectively, demonstrating a dominant pseudocapacity-controlled behavior in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5b</a>). In addition, the peak currents of CV curves were obviously enhanced and slightly right-shifted with the increase of scanning rate, which suggested that the currents were consisted of both surface-controlled and diffusion-controlled parts. Seen from the contribution ratio that was shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5c</a>, the capacitive behavior of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode was gradually enhanced and reached to 84% at 0.5&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurement was applied to assess the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients and further disclose the intrinsic diffusion kinetics of the as-prepared anode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5d</a>). Apparently, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C presented the highest diffusion coefficient compared with the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anodes. The Nyquist plots of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anodes were collected and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5e</a>. The semicircles in the high-mid frequency and the inclined lines in the low frequency regions respectively represented charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior. As a result, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode delivered the smallest R<sub>ct</sub> and the highest slope (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) versus the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, suggesting the superior charge transfer and ion diffusion. According to the Z'-ω<sup>−1/2</sup> fitting linear for the low-frequency region, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode exhibited the smallest Warburg impedance coefficient among the as-prepared anode materials (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0005">5f</a>), further verifying its excellent Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e5ff214c-16fe-43e4-a7c3-83f2bd7a0c41/smll202307986-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e5ff214c-16fe-43e4-a7c3-83f2bd7a0c41/smll202307986-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5f5621c8-cb78-4dda-891a-307035aab449/smll202307986-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e5ff214c-16fe-43e4-a7c3-83f2bd7a0c41/smll202307986-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The CV plots of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode at different scanning rates. b) b-values of redox peaks for cathode and anode scans. c) Capacitance contribution in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode. d) The comparison of GITT curves and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients for the as-prepared anodes. e) The comparison of Nyquist plots and f) plots of ω<sup>−1/2</sup> versus Z’ curves in the low-frequency region for the as-prepared anodes. Inset of e) is the equivalent circuit.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The COMSOL Multiphysics solver was utilized to simulate the structural advantage of nanorod–embedded porous network nanoarchitecture in the process of sodium insertion. The bare rod-like model, thin-layer carbon-coated model, and nanorod–embedded porous network model were built for comparison. Specifically, the porous network model (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6c</a>) displayed the lowest volume expansion compared with the bare model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6a</a>) and thin-layer carbon-coated model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6b</a>), according to the corresponding FEA results. The PPT shown in supporting information were dynamics simulation results of the models along with the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion from 0 to 200&nbsp;µs, which further confirmed the mild volume change in the porous network model. Seen from the calculated values from point A to B, a volume expansion rate of 42.8% was achieved for the nanorod–embedded porous network model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6f</a>), which was much better than 88.8% of the bare rod-like model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6d</a>) and 73.1% of the thin-layer carbon coated model (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6e</a>). Based on the above 2D simulation and calculation results, it could be concluded that the construction of nanorod–embedded porous network nanoarchitecture was conductive to dissipate local strain over the active materials, thus preventing the volumetric expansion and preserving the structural integrity of active materials. Accordingly, the digital photograph and corresponding FESEM images of the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C, MoS<sub>2</sub>/C, and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrodes were collected to investigate their surficial information after cycling. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6g</a>, the active materials onto the MoO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode film delivered a large deformation of agglomeration and breakage, even leading to the exposure of copper substrate. In contrast, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/C and MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrodes with porous network structure could maintain the smooth and tight surface on the substrate after inserting and taking off sodium ion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0006">6h,i</a>), which was consistent with the FEA simulation results. Based on the above analysis, the porous network presented impressive structure advantage in preventing volume collapse, which could endow the nanorod–embedded MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode with excellent cycling stability during the charge and discharge process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b301f22f-7f1f-4802-bfc3-51136288b62a/smll202307986-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b301f22f-7f1f-4802-bfc3-51136288b62a/smll202307986-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eeb5464b-d3db-42db-abe4-bbf748a7c556/smll202307986-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b301f22f-7f1f-4802-bfc3-51136288b62a/smll202307986-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">2D simulation results and corresponding volume change rates of a,d) the bare rod-like model, b,e) the thin-layer carbon coated model, and c,f) nanorod–embedded porous network model. Digital photographs and FESEM images of (g) MoO<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, h) MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, and i) MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C electrode, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>DFT calculations were conducted to investigate the sodium storage mechanism of MoO<sub>2</sub>, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>. The electronic structures were evaluated by partially density of states (PDOS) of Mo 3d orbit (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202307986-fig-0007">7a</a>), and the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> with d-band center value of 0.21&nbsp;eV presented an upshift toward the Fermi level compared to the 0.57&nbsp;eV of MoO<sub>2</sub> and 0.43&nbsp;eV of MoS<sub>2</sub>. According to the comparison for the total DOS shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> could possess higher occupied states near the Fermi Level in connection with excellent electron transfer ability. Furthermore, the charge of 0.64 |e| was transferred from MoS<sub>2</sub> to MoO<sub>2</sub> by the Bader analysis (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0007">7b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> achieved an adsorption energy value of 22123.08&nbsp;eV, which was more negative than the MoO<sub>2</sub> (−2.21&nbsp;eV) and MoS<sub>2</sub> (−2.47&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0007">7c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the electronic structure analysis, construction of MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> with both heterointerface and sulfur vacancy could not only improve the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> but also facilitate the mobility of electron, thereby enhancing the thermodynamic adsorption and electronic conduction. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202307986-fig-0007">7d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information) showed the optimized Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion paths in the MoO<sub>2</sub>, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>. Accordingly, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers were calculated to be 0.36&nbsp;eV for the MoS<sub>2</sub> and 0.08&nbsp;eV for the MoO<sub>2</sub>, respectively. Interestingly, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> delivered an energy barrier of 0.15&nbsp;eV meaning the toilless Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. We further calculated the theoretical capacity of the heterostructured unit cell by DFT to evaluate the capacity contribution of the different components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202307986-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202307986-bib-0021_R_d127202406e1914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc. Chem. Phys. Lett. NPJ Comput. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22–S38</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the number of containable Na atoms in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub> cell was 15, which was corresponding to a theoretical capacity of 157.04&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Based on the number of Na atoms in the MoO<sub>2</sub>, the rate of capacity contribution of MoO<sub>2</sub> could be ca. 80% in the heterostructure. Based on the above comparison, it was concluded that the heterostructure could combine the both advantages of thermodynamic adsorption in the vacancy–enriched MoS<sub>2</sub> and kinetic migration capacity of Na<sup>+</sup> in the MoO<sub>2</sub> thus improving the rate-performances.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202307986-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5053162d-e121-4793-a05e-51e8038a0f73/smll202307986-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5053162d-e121-4793-a05e-51e8038a0f73/smll202307986-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/78c9d382-e088-49f1-bc43-fd830a8b4478/smll202307986-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5053162d-e121-4793-a05e-51e8038a0f73/smll202307986-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202307986-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202307986" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) PDOS of Mo 3d orbit in the MoO<sub>2</sub>, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>. b) Electron density difference in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>. c) The comparison for the adsorption energies of Na<sup>+</sup> in the MoO<sub>2</sub>, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>. d) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path in the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>. e) The comparison for diffusion energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in the MoO<sub>2</sub>, MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202307986-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202307986-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we reported an “embryo” strategy to construct the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C porous network with the assistance of trifunctional L-cysteine and metastable monoclinic MoO<sub>3</sub> nanorods for robust SIBs. Enough hydrogen-deficient L-cysteine and MoO<sub>x</sub> analogues can be pre-encapsulated in the “embryo” precursors at RT, and the sulfuration, reduction, and carbonization can be realized via the subsequent multistep heating treatment, thus in situ generating the nanorod–embedded and sulfur vacancy–enriched MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C nanoarchitecture. Benefiting from the particular nanorod–embedded network structure, the volume expansion is reduced of ca. 50% in the nanoarchitecture model compared with that in the single nanorod model, according to the FEA results. The DFT calculations signify that the thermodynamic adsorption and kinetic migration capacity of Na<sup>+</sup> could be highly promoted through coupling sulfur vacancy and heterostructure. The MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C presented much higher rate capacity than the most reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes, especially at current densities higher than 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the MoO<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>/C anode delivers capacity retention rates of 82.1% (vs 2nd cycle) and 86.4% (vs 10th cycle) after cycling for 4000 and 300 times at 0.5 and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. This work could provide an inspiration for the design of vacancy–enriched heterostructure as anode materials with high-rate capability and long-term durability.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="smll202307986-bib-0003"><span class="bullet">3</span>a) <span class="author">K. Chayambuka</span>, <span class="author">G. Mulder</span>, <span class="author">D. L.
